href="/wiki/Kenny_Williams_(baseball)" title="Kenny Williams (baseball)">Kenny Williams</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Chicago White Sox</b> are an American professional <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a> team based in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago, Illinois</a>. The White Sox compete in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> (MLB) as a member club of the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> (AL) <a href="/wiki/American_League_Central" title="American League Central">Central</a> division. The White Sox are owned by <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Reinsdorf" title="Jerry Reinsdorf">Jerry Reinsdorf</a>, and play their home games at <a href="/wiki/Guaranteed_Rate_Field" title="Guaranteed Rate Field">Guaranteed Rate Field</a>, located on the city\'s <a href="/wiki/South_Side,_Chicago" title="South Side, Chicago">South Side</a>. They are one of two major league clubs in Chicago; the other is the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a>, who are a member of the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> (NL) <a href="/wiki/National_League_Central" title="National League Central">Central</a> division.\n</p><p>One of the American League\'s eight charter <a href="/wiki/Major_North_American_professional_sports_teams" class="mw-redirect" title="Major North American professional sports teams">franchises</a>, the franchise was established as a major league baseball club in <a href="/wiki/1901_in_baseball" title="1901 in baseball">1901</a>. The club was originally called the Chicago White Stockings, but this was soon shortened to Chicago White Sox. The team originally played home games at <a href="/wiki/South_Side_Park" title="South Side Park">South Side Park</a> before moving to <a href="/wiki/Comiskey_Park" title="Comiskey Park">Comiskey Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/1910_in_baseball" title="1910 in baseball">1910</a>, where they played until Guaranteed Rate Field (originally known as Comiskey Park and then known as <a href="/wiki/U.S._Cellular" title="U.S. Cellular">U.S. Cellular</a> Field) opened in 1991.\n</p><p>The White Sox won the <a href="/wiki/1906_World_Series" title="1906 World Series">1906 World Series</a> with a defense-oriented team dubbed "the Hitless Wonders", and the <a href="/wiki/1917_World_Series" title="1917 World Series">1917 World Series</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Cicotte" title="Eddie Cicotte">Eddie Cicotte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Collins" title="Eddie Collins">Eddie Collins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shoeless_Joe_Jackson" title="Shoeless Joe Jackson">Shoeless Joe Jackson</a>.  The <a href="/wiki/1919_World_Series" title="1919 World Series">1919 World Series</a> was marred by the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sox_Scandal" title="Black Sox Scandal">Black Sox Scandal</a>, in which several members of the White Sox were accused of conspiring with gamblers to <a href="/wiki/Match_fixing" title="Match fixing">fix games</a>. In response, Major League Baseball\'s new <a href="/wiki/Commissioner_of_Baseball" title="Commissioner of Baseball">Commissioner</a> <a href="/wiki/Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis" title="Kenesaw Mountain Landis">Kenesaw Mountain Landis</a> banned the players from Major League Baseball for life.  In <a href="/wiki/1959_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1959 Chicago White Sox season">1959</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Early_Wynn" title="Early Wynn">Early Wynn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nellie_Fox" title="Nellie Fox">Nellie Fox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luis_Aparicio" title="Luis Aparicio">Luis Aparicio</a> and manager <a href="/wiki/Al_L%C3%B3pez" title="Al L\xc3\xb3pez">Al L\xc3\xb3pez</a>, the White Sox won the <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_League_pennant_winners" title="List of American League pennant winners">American League pennant</a>. They won the AL pennant in <a href="/wiki/2005_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="2005 Chicago White Sox season">2005</a>, and went on to win the <a href="/wiki/2005_World_Series" title="2005 World Series">World Series</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Konerko" title="Paul Konerko">Paul Konerko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Buehrle" title="Mark Buehrle">Mark Buehrle</a>, catcher <a href="/wiki/A._J._Pierzynski" title="A. J. Pierzynski">A. J. Pierzynski</a>, and the first <a href="/wiki/Latino" title="Latino">Latino</a> manager to win the World Series, <a href="/wiki/Ozzie_Guill%C3%A9n" title="Ozzie Guill\xc3\xa9n">Ozzie Guill\xc3\xa9n</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Ballparks"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Ballparks</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Previous_ballparks"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Previous ballparks</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Spring_training_ballparks"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Spring training ballparks</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Logos_and_uniforms"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Logos and uniforms</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Nicknames"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Nicknames</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Mascots"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Mascots</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Fight_and_theme_songs"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Fight and theme songs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Rivalries"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Rivalries</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-11"><a href="#Crosstown_Classic"><span class="tocnumber">4.4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Crosstown Classic</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-12"><a href="#Divisional"><span class="tocnumber">4.4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Divisional</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-13"><a href="#Historical"><span class="tocnumber">4.4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Historical</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Broadcasting"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Broadcasting</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Radio"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Radio</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Television"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Television</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Personnel"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Personnel</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Current_roster"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Current roster</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Front_office_and_key_personnel"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Front office and key personnel</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Awards_and_accolades"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Awards and accolades</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#World_Series_championships"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">World Series championships</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#American_League_championships"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">American League championships</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Award_winners"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Award winners</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Team_captains"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Team captains</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Retired_numbers"><span class="tocnumber">7.5</span> <span class="toctext">Retired numbers</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-26"><a href="#Out_of_circulation,_but_not_retired"><span class="tocnumber">7.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Out of circulation, but not retired</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Baseball_Hall_of_Famers"><span class="tocnumber">7.6</span> <span class="toctext">Baseball Hall of Famers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Ford_C._Frick_Award_recipients"><span class="tocnumber">7.7</span> <span class="toctext">Ford C. Frick Award recipients</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Minor_league_affiliates"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Minor league affiliates</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Silver_Chalice_subsidiary"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Silver Chalice subsidiary</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chicago_White_Sox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Chicago_White_Sox" title="History of the Chicago White Sox">History of the Chicago White Sox</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:265px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1906_Chicago_White_Sox.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/1906_Chicago_White_Sox.jpg/263px-1906_Chicago_White_Sox.jpg" width="263" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/1906_Chicago_White_Sox.jpg/395px-1906_Chicago_White_Sox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/1906_Chicago_White_Sox.jpg/526px-1906_Chicago_White_Sox.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1906_Chicago_White_Sox.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1906 White Sox, with club founder Charles Comiskey</div></div></div>\n<p>The White Sox originated as the Sioux City Cornhuskers of the <a href="/wiki/Western_League_(original)" class="mw-redirect" title="Western League (original)">Western League</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Minor_league" title="Minor league">minor league</a> under the parameters of the National Agreement with the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a>. In 1894, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Comiskey" title="Charles Comiskey">Charles Comiskey</a> bought the Cornhuskers and moved them to <a href="/wiki/St._Paul,_Minnesota" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Paul, Minnesota">St. Paul, Minnesota</a>, where they became the <a href="/wiki/St._Paul_Saints" title="St. Paul Saints">St. Paul Saints</a>. In 1900, with the approval of Western League president <a href="/wiki/Ban_Johnson" title="Ban Johnson">Ban Johnson</a>, Charles Comiskey moved the Saints into his hometown neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Armour_Square,_Chicago" title="Armour Square, Chicago">Armour Square, Chicago</a>, where they became known as the <b>White Stockings,</b> the former name of Chicago\'s National League team, the Orphans (now the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/1901_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1901 Major League Baseball season">1901</a>, the Western League broke the National Agreement and became the new major league <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a>. The very first season in the American League ended with a White Stockings championship.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> However, that would be the end of the season as the <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> did not begin until <a href="/wiki/1903_World_Series" title="1903 World Series">1903</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The franchise, now known as the <b>Chicago White Sox</b>, made its first World Series appearance in <a href="/wiki/1906_World_Series" title="1906 World Series">1906</a>, beating the crosstown Cubs in six games.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The White Sox would win a third pennant and second World Series in <a href="/wiki/1917_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1917 Chicago White Sox season">1917</a>, beating the <a href="/wiki/1917_New_York_Giants_season" title="1917 New York Giants season">New York Giants</a> in six games with help from stars <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Cicotte" title="Eddie Cicotte">Eddie Cicotte</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shoeless_Joe" class="mw-redirect" title="Shoeless Joe">"Shoeless" Joe Jackson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The Sox were heavily favored in the <a href="/wiki/1919_World_Series" title="1919 World Series">1919 World Series</a>, but lost to the <a href="/wiki/1919_Cincinnati_Reds_season" title="1919 Cincinnati Reds season">Cincinnati Reds</a> in 8 games. Huge bets on the Reds fueled speculation that the series had been <a href="/wiki/Match_fixing" title="Match fixing">fixed</a>. A criminal investigation went on in the 1920 season, and though all players were acquitted, commissioner <a href="/wiki/Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis" title="Kenesaw Mountain Landis">Kenesaw Mountain Landis</a> banned eight of the White Sox players for life, in what was known as the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sox_Scandal" title="Black Sox Scandal">Black Sox Scandal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> This set the franchise back, as they did not win another pennant for 40 years.\n</p><p>The White Sox did not finish in the upper half of the American League again until after club founder Charles Comiskey died and passed ownership of the club to his son, <a href="/wiki/J._Louis_Comiskey" title="J. Louis Comiskey">J. Louis Comiskey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> They finished in the upper half most years between 1936\xe2\x80\x931946 under the leadership of manager <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Dykes" title="Jimmy Dykes">Jimmy Dykes</a>, with star shortstop <a href="/wiki/Luke_Appling" title="Luke Appling">Luke Appling</a>, known as Ol\' Aches and Pains, and pitcher <a href="/wiki/Ted_Lyons" title="Ted Lyons">Ted Lyons</a>. Appling and Lyons have their numbers 4 and 16 retired.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After J. Louis Comiskey died in 1939, ownership of the club was passed down to his widow, <a href="/wiki/Grace_Comiskey" title="Grace Comiskey">Grace Comiskey</a>. The club was later passed down to Grace\'s children <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Comiskey_Rigney" title="Dorothy Comiskey Rigney">Dorothy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Comiskey" title="Chuck Comiskey">Chuck</a> in 1956, with Dorothy selling a majority share to a group led by <a href="/wiki/Bill_Veeck" title="Bill Veeck">Bill Veeck</a> after the <a href="/wiki/1958_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1958 Chicago White Sox season">1958 season</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Veeck was notorious for his promotional stunts, attracting fans to <a href="/wiki/Comiskey_Park" title="Comiskey Park">Comiskey Park</a> with the new "exploding scoreboard" and outfield shower. In 1961, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Allyn,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur Allyn, Jr.">Arthur Allyn, Jr.</a> briefly owned the club before selling to his brother <a href="/wiki/John_Allyn" title="John Allyn">John Allyn</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Al_Lopez_-_WJROneOfAKind.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Al_Lopez_-_WJROneOfAKind.jpg/200px-Al_Lopez_-_WJROneOfAKind.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Al_Lopez_-_WJROneOfAKind.jpg/300px-Al_Lopez_-_WJROneOfAKind.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Al_Lopez_-_WJROneOfAKind.jpg/400px-Al_Lopez_-_WJROneOfAKind.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Al_Lopez_-_WJROneOfAKind.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Al_L%C3%B3pez" title="Al L\xc3\xb3pez">Al L\xc3\xb3pez</a>, manager of the Go-Go White Sox</div></div></div>\n<p>From 1951 to 1967, the White Sox had their longest period of sustained success, scoring a winning record for 17 straight seasons. Known as the "Go-Go White Sox" for their tendency to focus on speed and getting on base versus power hitting, they featured stars such as <a href="/wiki/Minnie_Mi%C3%B1oso" title="Minnie Mi\xc3\xb1oso">Minnie Mi\xc3\xb1oso</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nellie_Fox" title="Nellie Fox">Nellie Fox</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Luis_Aparicio" title="Luis Aparicio">Luis Aparicio</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Billy_Pierce" title="Billy Pierce">Billy Pierce</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sherm_Lollar" title="Sherm Lollar">Sherm Lollar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> From 1957 to 1965, the Sox were managed by <a href="/wiki/Al_L%C3%B3pez" title="Al L\xc3\xb3pez">Al L\xc3\xb3pez</a>. The Sox finished in the upper half of the American League in eight of his nine seasons, including six years in the top two of the league.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/1959_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1959 Chicago White Sox season">1959</a>, the White Sox ended the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a> dominance over the American League, and won their first pennant since the ill-fated 1919 campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Despite winning game one of the <a href="/wiki/1959_World_Series" title="1959 World Series">1959 World Series</a> 11-0, they fell to the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a> in six games.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The late 1960s and 70s were a tumultuous time for the Sox, as they struggled to win games and attract fans. Allyn and <a href="/wiki/Bud_Selig" title="Bud Selig">Bud Selig</a> agreed to a handshake deal that would give Selig control of the club and move them to <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee" title="Milwaukee">Milwaukee</a>; however, this was blocked by the American League.<sup id="cite_ref-BovaSOS_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BovaSOS-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Selig instead bought the <a href="/wiki/Seattle_Pilots" title="Seattle Pilots">Seattle Pilots</a> and moved them to Milwaukee, putting enormous pressure on the American League to place a team in <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>. A plan was in place for the Sox to move to Seattle and for <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Finley" title="Charlie Finley">Charlie Finley</a> to move his <a href="/wiki/Oakland_A%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="Oakland A&#39;s">Oakland A\'s</a> to Chicago. However, Chicago had a renewed interest in the Sox after the 1972 season, and the American League instead added the expansion <a href="/wiki/Seattle_Mariners" title="Seattle Mariners">Seattle Mariners</a>. The <a href="/wiki/1972_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1972 Chicago White Sox season">1972 White Sox</a>  were one of the lone successful season of this era, as <a href="/wiki/Dick_Allen" title="Dick Allen">Dick Allen</a> wound up winning the American League MVP award.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Some have said that Dick Allen is responsible for saving the White Sox in Chicago. Bill Veeck returned as owner of the Sox in 1975, and despite not having much money, they managed to win 90 games in <a href="/wiki/1977_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1977 Chicago White Sox season">1977</a>, a team known as the South Side Hitmen.\n</p><p>However, the team\'s fortunes plummeted after the 1977 season, plagued by 90-loss teams and scarred by the notorious <a href="/wiki/Disco_Demolition_Night" title="Disco Demolition Night">Disco Demolition Night</a> promotion in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Bill Veeck was forced to sell the team. He rejected offers from ownership groups intent on moving the club to <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a>, eventually agreeing to sell the club to <a href="/wiki/Edward_J._DeBartolo,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Edward J. DeBartolo, Sr.">Ed DeBartolo</a>, who was the only prospective owner who promised to keep the Sox in Chicago. However, DeBartolo was rejected by the owners, and the club was then sold to a group headed by <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Reinsdorf" title="Jerry Reinsdorf">Jerry Reinsdorf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Einhorn" title="Eddie Einhorn">Eddie Einhorn</a>. The Reinsdorf era started off well, as the Sox won their first division title in <a href="/wiki/1983_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1983 Chicago White Sox season">1983</a>, led by manager <a href="/wiki/Tony_La_Russa" title="Tony La Russa">Tony La Russa</a><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and stars <a href="/wiki/Carlton_Fisk" title="Carlton Fisk">Carlton Fisk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Paciorek" title="Tom Paciorek">Tom Paciorek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ron_Kittle" title="Ron Kittle">Ron Kittle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Baines" title="Harold Baines">Harold Baines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/LaMarr_Hoyt" title="LaMarr Hoyt">LaMarr Hoyt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> During the 1986 season, La Russa was fired by announcer-turned-GM <a href="/wiki/Ken_Harrelson" title="Ken Harrelson">Ken Harrelson</a>. La Russa went on to manage in six World Series (winning 3) with the <a href="/wiki/Oakland_A%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="Oakland A&#39;s">Oakland A\'s</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Cardinals</a>, ending up in the <a href="/wiki/National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="National Baseball Hall of Fame">Hall of Fame</a> as the third-winningest manager of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frank_Thomas_1997.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Frank_Thomas_1997.jpg/220px-Frank_Thomas_1997.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Frank_Thomas_1997.jpg/330px-Frank_Thomas_1997.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Frank_Thomas_1997.jpg/440px-Frank_Thomas_1997.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4119" data-file-height="2731" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frank_Thomas_1997.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frank Thomas in 1997</div></div></div>\n<p>The White Sox struggled for the rest of the 1980s, as Chicago fought to keep the Sox in town. Reinsdorf wanted to replace the aging <a href="/wiki/Comiskey_Park" title="Comiskey Park">Comiskey Park</a>, and sought public funds to do so. When talks stalled, there was a strong offer to move the team to the <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa, Florida</a> area.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Funding for a new ballpark was approved in an 11th hour deal by the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_State_Legislature" class="mw-redirect" title="Illinois State Legislature">Illinois State Legislature</a> on June 30, 1988, with the stipulation that new park had to be built on the corner of 35th and Shields, across the street from the old ballpark, as opposed to the suburban ballpark the owners had designed.<sup id="cite_ref-BovaSOS_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BovaSOS-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Architects offered to redesign the ballpark to a more "retro" feel that would fit in the city blocks around Comiskey Park; however, the ownership group was set on a 1991 open date, and so they kept the old design.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In 1991, the <a href="/wiki/New_Comiskey_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="New Comiskey Park">new Comiskey Park</a> opened. However, it would be rendered obsolete a year later with the opening of the retro-inspired <a href="/wiki/Oriole_Park_at_Camden_Yards" title="Oriole Park at Camden Yards">Oriole Park at Camden Yards</a>. The park, now known as <a href="/wiki/Guaranteed_Rate_Field" title="Guaranteed Rate Field">Guaranteed Rate Field</a>, underwent many renovations in the early 2000s to give it a more retro feel.\n</p><p>The White Sox were fairly successful in the 1990s and early 2000s, with 12 winning seasons between 1990\xe2\x80\x932005. First Baseman <a href="/wiki/Frank_Thomas_(designated_hitter)" title="Frank Thomas (designated hitter)">Frank Thomas</a> became the face of the franchise, ending his career as the White Sox\' all-time leader in runs, doubles, home runs, total bases and walks.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Other major players included <a href="/wiki/Robin_Ventura" title="Robin Ventura">Robin Ventura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ozzie_Guill%C3%A9n" title="Ozzie Guill\xc3\xa9n">Ozzie Guill\xc3\xa9n</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_McDowell" title="Jack McDowell">Jack McDowell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Thigpen" title="Bobby Thigpen">Bobby Thigpen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The Sox would win the West division in <a href="/wiki/1993_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1993 Chicago White Sox season">1993</a>, and were in first place in <a href="/wiki/1994_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1994 Chicago White Sox season">1994</a> when the season was cancelled due to the <a href="/wiki/1994_MLB_Strike" class="mw-redirect" title="1994 MLB Strike">1994 MLB Strike</a>.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/2004_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="2004 Chicago White Sox season">2004</a>, Ozzie Guill\xc3\xa9n was hired as manager of his former team.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> After finishing second in 2004, the Sox won 99 games and the Central Division title in <a href="/wiki/2005_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="2005 Chicago White Sox season">2005</a> behind the work of stars <a href="/wiki/Paul_Konerko" title="Paul Konerko">Paul Konerko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Buehrle" title="Mark Buehrle">Mark Buehrle</a>, <a href="/wiki/A._J._Pierzynski" title="A. J. Pierzynski">A. J. Pierzynski</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Crede" title="Joe Crede">Joe Crede</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Orlando_Hern%C3%A1ndez" title="Orlando Hern\xc3\xa1ndez">Orlando Hern\xc3\xa1ndez</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> They started the playoffs by sweeping the defending champion <a href="/wiki/2005_Boston_Red_Sox_season" title="2005 Boston Red Sox season">Boston Red Sox</a> in the <a href="/wiki/ALDS" class="mw-redirect" title="ALDS">ALDS</a>, and then beat the <a href="/wiki/2005_Los_Angeles_Angels_of_Anaheim_season" class="mw-redirect" title="2005 Los Angeles Angels of Anaheim season">Angels</a> in 5 games to win their first pennant in 46 years, thanks to 4 complete games by the White Sox rotation.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The White Sox went on to sweep the <a href="/wiki/2005_Houston_Astros_season" title="2005 Houston Astros season">Houston Astros</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2005_World_Series" title="2005 World Series">2005 World Series</a>, giving the Sox their first World Championship in 88 years.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Guillen had marginal success during the rest of his tenure, with the Sox winning the Central Division title in <a href="/wiki/2008_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="2008 Chicago White Sox season">2008</a> after a <a href="/wiki/2008_American_League_Central_tie-breaker_game" title="2008 American League Central tie-breaker game">one game playoff</a> with the <a href="/wiki/2008_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2008 Minnesota Twins season">Minnesota Twins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> However, Guillen left the White Sox after the <a href="/wiki/2011_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="2011 Chicago White Sox season">2011 season</a>, and was replaced by former teammate <a href="/wiki/Robin_Ventura" title="Robin Ventura">Robin Ventura</a>. The White Sox finished the <a href="/wiki/2015_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="2015 Chicago White Sox season">2015 season</a>, their 115th in Chicago, with a 76-86 record, a 3-game improvement over <a href="/wiki/2014_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="2014 Chicago White Sox season">2014</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The White Sox recorded their 9000th win in franchise history against the home team Detroit by the score of 3-2 on Monday, September 21, 2015. Ventura returned in 2016, with a young core featuring <a href="/wiki/Jose_Abreu_(baseball)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jose Abreu (baseball)">Jose Abreu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adam_Eaton_(outfielder)" title="Adam Eaton (outfielder)">Adam Eaton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Quintana" title="Jos\xc3\xa9 Quintana">Jos\xc3\xa9 Quintana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Sale" title="Chris Sale">Chris Sale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Ventura resigned after the 2016 season in which the White Sox finished 78-84. <a href="/wiki/Rick_Renteria" title="Rick Renteria">Rick Renteria</a>, the 2016 White Sox <a href="/wiki/Bench_coach" class="mw-redirect" title="Bench coach">bench coach</a>, was promoted to the role of manager.\n</p><p>Prior to the start of the 2017 season, the White Sox traded <a href="/wiki/Chris_Sale" title="Chris Sale">Chris Sale</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Eaton_(outfielder)" title="Adam Eaton (outfielder)">Adam Eaton</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Nationals" title="Washington Nationals">Washington Nationals</a> for prospects including <a href="/wiki/Yoan_Moncada" title="Yoan Moncada">Yoan Moncada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Giolito" title="Lucas Giolito">Lucas Giolito</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Kopech" title="Michael Kopech">Michael Kopech</a>, signaling the beginning of a rebuilding period.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ballparks">Ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chicago_White_Sox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Guaranteed_Rate_Field" title="Guaranteed Rate Field">Guaranteed Rate Field</a></div>\n<p>In the late 1980s, the franchise threatened to relocate to <a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay_Area" title="Tampa Bay Area">Tampa Bay</a> (as did the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Giants" title="San Francisco Giants">San Francisco Giants</a>), but frantic<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> lobbying on the part of the Illinois governor <a href="/wiki/James_R._Thompson" title="James R. Thompson">James R. Thompson</a> and state legislature resulted in approval (by one vote) of public funding for a new stadium.  Designed primarily as a baseball stadium (as opposed to a "multipurpose" stadium) New Comiskey Park (redubbed U.S. Cellular Field in 2003 and <a href="/wiki/Guaranteed_Rate_Field" title="Guaranteed Rate Field">Guaranteed Rate Field</a> in 2016) was built in a 1960s style similar to <a href="/wiki/Dodger_Stadium" title="Dodger Stadium">Dodger Stadium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kauffman_Stadium" title="Kauffman Stadium">Kauffman Stadium</a>. It opened in <a href="/wiki/1991_in_baseball" title="1991 in baseball">1991</a> to positive reviews; many praised its wide open concourses, excellent sight lines, and natural grass (unlike other stadiums of the era such as <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Centre" title="Rogers Centre">Rogers Centre</a> in Toronto). The park\'s inaugural season drew 2,934,154 fans&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 at the time, an all-time attendance record for any Chicago baseball team.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Uscellular.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Uscellular.jpg/250px-Uscellular.jpg" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Uscellular.jpg/375px-Uscellular.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Uscellular.jpg/500px-Uscellular.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1023" data-file-height="680" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Uscellular.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View from the upper deck of U.S. Cellular Field in 2006</div></div></div>\n<p>In recent years, money accrued from the sale of naming rights to <a href="/wiki/U.S._Cellular" title="U.S. Cellular">U.S. Cellular</a> has been allocated for renovations to make the park more aesthetically appealing and fan friendly. Notable renovations of early phases included: re-orientation of the bullpens parallel to the field of play (thus decreasing slightly the formerly symmetrical dimensions of the outfield); filling seats in up to and shortening the outfield wall; ballooning foul-line seat sections out toward the field of play; creating a new multi-tiered <a href="/wiki/Batter%27s_eye" title="Batter&#39;s eye">batter\'s eye</a>, allowing fans to see out through one-way screens from the center-field vantage point, and complete with concession stand and bar-style seating on its \'fan deck\'; renovating all concourse areas with brick, historic murals, and new concession stand ornaments to establish a more friendly feel. The stadium\'s steel and concrete was repainted dark gray and black. The scoreboard Jumbotron was also replaced with a new <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi" title="Mitsubishi">Mitsubishi</a> Diamondvision <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">HDTV</a> giant screen.\n</p><p>More recently, the top quarter of the upper deck was removed in <a href="/wiki/2004_in_baseball" title="2004 in baseball">2004</a> and a black wrought metal roof was placed over it, covering all but the first eight rows of seats. This decreased <a href="/wiki/Seating_capacity" title="Seating capacity">seating capacity</a> from 47,098 to 40,615. 2005 also saw the introduction of the Scout Seats, redesignating (and re-upholstering) 200 lower deck seats behind home plate as an exclusive area, with seat-side waitstaff and a complete restaurant located underneath the concourse. The most significant structural addition besides the new roof was <a href="/wiki/2005_in_baseball" title="2005 in baseball">2005</a>\'s FUNdamentals Deck, a multi-tiered structure on the left field concourse containing batting cages, a small <a href="/wiki/Tee_Ball" class="mw-redirect" title="Tee Ball">Tee Ball</a> field, speed pitch, and several other child-themed activities intended to entertain and educate young fans with the help of coaching staff from the Chicago Bulls/Sox Training Academy. This structure was used during the 2005 playoffs by <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox Broadcasting Company</a> as a broadcasting platform.\n</p><p>Designed as a 7-phase plan, the renovations were completed before the <a href="/wiki/2007_in_baseball" title="2007 in baseball">2007</a> season with the 7th and final phase. The most visible renovation in this final phase was replacing the original blue seats with green seats. The upper deck already had new green seats, put in before the beginning of the 2006 season. Beginning with the <a href="/wiki/2007_in_baseball" title="2007 in baseball">2007</a> season a new luxury seating section was added in the former press box. This section has amenities similar to those of the Scout Seats section. After the 2007 season the ballpark continued renovation projects despite that the 7-phase plan was complete.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Previous_ballparks">Previous ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chicago_White_Sox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Previous ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:00118_n_9abwn9hwm1496.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/00118_n_9abwn9hwm1496.jpg/250px-00118_n_9abwn9hwm1496.jpg" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/00118_n_9abwn9hwm1496.jpg/375px-00118_n_9abwn9hwm1496.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/00118_n_9abwn9hwm1496.jpg/500px-00118_n_9abwn9hwm1496.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4189" data-file-height="2775" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:00118_n_9abwn9hwm1496.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Batting practice at Comiskey Park in 1986</div></div></div>\n<p>The St. Paul Saints first played their games at <a href="/wiki/Lexington_Park" title="Lexington Park">Lexington Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> When they moved to Chicago\'s <a href="/wiki/Armour_Square" class="mw-redirect" title="Armour Square">Armour Square</a> neighborhood, they began play at the <a href="/wiki/South_Side_Park_III" class="mw-redirect" title="South Side Park III">South Side Park</a>. Previously a <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a> ground, the park was located on the north side of 39th Street (now called Pershing Road) between South Wentworth and South Princeton Avenues.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Its massive dimensions yielded few home runs, which was to the advantage of the White Sox\' <a href="/wiki/1906_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1906 Chicago White Sox season">Hitless Wonders</a> teams of the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the 1909 season, the Sox moved 5 blocks to the north to play in the new <a href="/wiki/Comiskey_Park" title="Comiskey Park">Comiskey Park</a>, while the 39th Street grounds became the home of the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_American_Giants" title="Chicago American Giants">Chicago American Giants</a> of the Negro Leagues. Billed as the Baseball Palace of the World, it originally held 28,000 seats and eventually grew to hold over 50,000.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> It became known for its many odd features, such as the outdoor shower and the exploding scoreboard. When it closed after the <a href="/wiki/1990_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1990 Chicago White Sox season">1990 season</a>, it was the oldest ballpark still in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spring_training_ballparks">Spring training ballparks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chicago_White_Sox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Spring training ballparks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The White Sox have held spring training in:<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div>\n\n&#32;\n<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"><tbody><tr>\n<td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Excelsior_Springs,_Missouri" title="Excelsior Springs, Missouri">Excelsior Springs, Missouri</a> (1901\xe2\x80\x931902)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mobile,_Alabama" title="Mobile, Alabama">Mobile, Alabama</a> (1903);</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Marlin,_Texas" title="Marlin, Texas">Marlin Springs, Texas</a> (1904)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> (1905\xe2\x80\x931906)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>, Mexico (1907)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> (1908)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> (<a href="/wiki/Recreation_Park_(San_Francisco)" title="Recreation Park (San Francisco)">Recreation Park</a>, 1909\xe2\x80\x931910)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mineral_Wells,_Texas" title="Mineral Wells, Texas">Mineral Wells, Texas</a> (1911, 1916\xe2\x80\x931919)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Waco,_Texas" title="Waco, Texas">Waco, Texas</a> (1912, 1920);</li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Paso_Robles,_California" title="Paso Robles, California">Paso Robles, California</a> (1913\xe2\x80\x931915)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Waxahachie,_Texas" title="Waxahachie, Texas">Waxahachie, Texas</a> (1921)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Seguin,_Texas" title="Seguin, Texas">Seguin, Texas</a> (1922\xe2\x80\x931923)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Winter_Haven,_Florida" title="Winter Haven, Florida">Winter Haven, Florida</a>. (1924)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Shreveport,_Louisiana" title="Shreveport, Louisiana">Shreveport, Louisiana</a> (1925\xe2\x80\x931928)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a> (1929)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a> (1930\xe2\x80\x931932)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Pasadena,_California" title="Pasadena, California">Pasadena, California</a> (1933\xe2\x80\x931942, 1946\xe2\x80\x931950)</li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/French_Lick,_Indiana" title="French Lick, Indiana">French Lick, Indiana</a> (1943\xe2\x80\x931944)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Terre_Haute,_Indiana" title="Terre Haute, Indiana">Terre Haute, Indiana</a> (1945)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Palm_Springs,_California" title="Palm Springs, California">Palm Springs, California</a> (<a href="/wiki/Palm_Springs_Stadium" title="Palm Springs Stadium">Palm Springs Stadium</a>, 1951)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/El_Centro,_California" title="El Centro, California">El Centro, California</a> (1952\xe2\x80\x931953);</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa, Florida</a> (1954\xe2\x80\x931959, <a href="/wiki/Plant_Field" title="Plant Field">Plant Field</a>, 1954, <a href="/wiki/Al_Lopez_Field" title="Al Lopez Field">Al Lopez Field</a> 1955\xe2\x80\x931959)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sarasota,_Florida" title="Sarasota, Florida">Sarasota, Florida</a> (1960\xe2\x80\x931997; <a href="/wiki/Payne_Park" title="Payne Park">Payne Park</a> <a href="/wiki/Ed_Smith_Stadium" title="Ed Smith Stadium">Ed Smith Stadium</a> 1989\xe2\x80\x9397).</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tucson,_Arizona" title="Tucson, Arizona">Tucson, Arizona</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tucson_Electric_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Tucson Electric Park">Tucson Electric Park</a>, 1998\xe2\x80\x932008, <a href="/wiki/Cactus_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Cactus League">Cactus League</a>, shared with <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Diamondbacks" title="Arizona Diamondbacks">Arizona Diamondbacks</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix, Arizona</a> (<a href="/wiki/Camelback_Ranch" title="Camelback Ranch">Camelback Ranch</a>, 2009\xe2\x80\x93present)</li></ul>\n<p>&#32;\n</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<p>On November 19, 2007, the cities of <a href="/wiki/Glendale,_Arizona" title="Glendale, Arizona">Glendale, Arizona</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix, Arizona</a> broke ground on the Cactus League\'s newest Spring Training facility. <a href="/wiki/Camelback_Ranch" title="Camelback Ranch">Camelback Ranch</a>, the $76 million two-team facility is the new home of both the White Sox and the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a> for their Spring Training programs. Aside from <a href="/wiki/State-of-the-art" class="mw-redirect" title="State-of-the-art">state-of-the-art</a> baseball facilities at the 10,000-seat stadium the location includes residential, restaurant and retail development, a 4-star hotel and 18-hole <a href="/wiki/Golf_course" title="Golf course">golf course</a>. Other amenities include 118,000&#160;sq&#160;ft (11,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) of Major and minor league clubhouses for the two teams, four Major League practice fields and eight minor league practice fields, two practice infields and parking to accommodate 5,000 vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Logos_and_uniforms">Logos and uniforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chicago_White_Sox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Logos and uniforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_uniforms" title="Major League Baseball uniforms">Major League Baseball uniforms</a></div>\n<p>Over the years the White Sox have become noted for many of their <a href="/wiki/Baseball_uniform" title="Baseball uniform">uniform</a> innovations and changes. In 1960, the White Sox became the first team in the major sports to put players\' last names on <a href="/wiki/Jersey_(clothing)#In_sports" title="Jersey (clothing)">jerseys</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Im_cws_logo_1912.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/66/Im_cws_logo_1912.png" width="120" height="120" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="120" data-file-height="120" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption">1912\xe2\x80\x931917, 1919\xe2\x80\x931929, 1931, and 1936\xe2\x80\x931938 Chicago White Sox logo</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1912 the White Sox debuted a large "S" in a Roman-style <a href="/wiki/Font" title="Font">font</a>, with a small "O" inside the top loop of the "S" and a small "X" inside the bottom loop. This is the logo associated with the 1917 World Series championship team and the 1919 Black Sox. With a couple of brief interruptions, the dark-blue logo with the large "S" lasted through 1938 (but continued in a modified block style into the 1940s). Through the 1940s, the White Sox team colors were primarily navy blue trimmed with red.\n</p><p>The White Sox logo in the 1950s and 1960s (actually beginning in the 1949 season) was the word "SOX" in an Old English font, diagonally arranged, with the "S" larger than the other two letters. From 1949 through 1963, the primary color was black (trimmed with red after 1951). The Old English "SOX" in black lettering is the logo associated with the Go-Go Sox era.\n</p><p>In 1964, the primary color went back to navy blue, and the road uniforms changed from gray to pale blue. In 1971, the team\'s primary color changed from royal blue to red, with the color of their <a href="/wiki/Pin_stripes" title="Pin stripes">pinstripes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baseball_cap" title="Baseball cap">caps</a> changing to red. The 1971\xe2\x80\x931975 uniform included red <a href="/wiki/Baseball_uniform#Stockings_and_pants" title="Baseball uniform">socks</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Sox_old_logo.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/32/White_Sox_old_logo.png" width="120" height="120" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="120" data-file-height="120" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption">Chicago White Sox logo (1976\xe2\x80\x931990)</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1976 the team\'s uniforms changed again. The team\'s primary color changed back from red to navy. The team based their uniforms on a style worn in the early days of the franchise, with white jerseys worn at home, blue on the <a href="/wiki/Road_(sports)" title="Road (sports)">road</a>. The team brought back white socks for the last time in team history. The socks featured a different stripe pattern every year. The team also had the option to wear blue or white pants with either jersey. Additionally the teams "SOX" logo was changed to a modern-looking "SOX" in a bold font, with \'CHICAGO\' written across the jersey. Finally, the team\'s logo featured a <a href="/wiki/Silhouette" title="Silhouette">silhouette</a> of a batter over the words "SOX".\n</p><p>The new uniforms also featured <a href="/wiki/Collar_(clothing)" title="Collar (clothing)">collars</a> and were designed to be worn untucked&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 both unprecedented. Yet by far the most unusual wrinkle was the option to wear shorts, which the White Sox did for the first game of a <a href="/wiki/Doubleheader_(baseball)" title="Doubleheader (baseball)">doubleheader</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Royals" title="Kansas City Royals">Kansas City Royals</a> in 1976. The <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Stars" title="Hollywood Stars">Hollywood Stars</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Coast_League" title="Pacific Coast League">Pacific Coast League</a> had previously tried the same concept, it was also poorly received. Apart from aesthetic issues, as a practical matter shorts are not conducive to sliding, due to the likelihood of significant abrasions.\n</p><p>Upon taking over the team in 1980 new owners <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Einhorn" title="Eddie Einhorn">Eddie Einhorn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Reinsdorf" title="Jerry Reinsdorf">Jerry Reinsdorf</a> announced a contest where fans were invited to create new uniforms for the White Sox. The winning entry was submitted by a fan where the word "SOX" was written across the front of the jersey, in the same font as a cap, inside of a large blue stripe trimmed with red. The red and blue stripes were also on the sleeves, and the road jerseys were gray to the home whites.  In those jerseys the White Sox won 99 games and the AL West championship in 1983, the best record in the majors.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Sox_Alt_Logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/White_Sox_Alt_Logo.svg/150px-White_Sox_Alt_Logo.svg.png" width="150" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/White_Sox_Alt_Logo.svg/225px-White_Sox_Alt_Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/White_Sox_Alt_Logo.svg/300px-White_Sox_Alt_Logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1456" data-file-height="1468" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Sox_Alt_Logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alternate logo, on road uniforms (1991\xe2\x80\x932010)</div></div></div>\n<p>After five years those uniforms were retired and replaced with a more basic uniform which had "White Sox" written across the front in script, with "Chicago" on the front of the road jersey. The cap logo was also changed to a cursive "C", although the batter logo was retained for several years.\n</p><p>For a mid-season 1990 game at Comiskey Park the White Sox appeared once in a uniform based on that of the 1917 White Sox.\n</p><p>The White Sox then switched their regular uniform style once more.  In September, for the final series at Old Comiskey Park, the old English "SOX" logo (a slightly simplified version of the 1949\xe2\x80\x9363 logo) was restored, and the new uniform also had the black pinstripes restored. The team\'s primary color changed back to black, this time with silver trim. The team also introduced a new sock logo\xe2\x80\x94a white silhouette of a sock centered inside a white outline of a baseball diamond\xe2\x80\x94which appeared as a sleeve patch on the away and alternate uniforms until 2011 when the patch was switched with the primary logo on the away uniform. With minor modifications (i.e., occasionally wearing vests, black game jerseys) the White Sox have used this style ever since.\n</p><p>During the 2012 and 2013 seasons, the White Sox wore their throwback uniforms at home every Sunday, starting with the 1972 red-pinstriped throwback jerseys worn during the 2012 season, followed by the 1981\xe2\x80\x9386 uniforms the next season. In the 2014 season, the "Winning Ugly" throwbacks were promoted to full-time alternate status, and is now worn at home on Sundays. In one game during the 2014 season, the White Sox paired their throwbacks with a cap featuring the batter logo instead of the wordmark "SOX"; this is currently their batting practice cap prior to games in the throwback uniforms.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chicago_White_Sox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicknames">Nicknames</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chicago_White_Sox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Nicknames">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The White Sox were originally known as the White Stockings, a reference to the original name of the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> To fit the name in headlines, local newspapers such as the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a> abbreviated the name alternatively to Stox and Sox.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Charles Comiskey would officially adopt the White Sox nickname in the club\'s first years, making them the first team to officially use the "Sox" name. The Chicago White Sox are most prominently <a href="/wiki/List_of_baseball_team_nicknames" title="List of baseball team nicknames">nicknamed</a> "the South Siders", based on their particular <a href="/wiki/South_Side,_Chicago" title="South Side, Chicago">district within Chicago</a>.  Other nicknames include the synonymous "Pale Hose";<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> "the ChiSox", a combination of "Chicago" and "Sox", used mostly by the national media to differentiate them between the Boston Red Sox (BoSox); and "the Good Guys", a reference to the team\'s one-time motto "Good guys wear black", coined by broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Ken_Harrelson" title="Ken Harrelson">Ken Harrelson</a>. Most fans and Chicago media refer to the team as simply "the Sox". The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish language</a> media sometimes refer to the team as <i>Medias Blancas</i> for "White Socks."\n</p><p>Several White Sox teams have received nicknames over the years:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/1906_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1906 Chicago White Sox season">1906 team</a> were known as the <b>Hitless Wonders,</b> due to their .230 batting average, worst in the American League.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Despite their hitting woes, the Sox would beat the crosstown Cubs for their first world title.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/1919_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1919 Chicago White Sox season">1919 White Sox</a> are known as the <b><a href="/wiki/Black_Sox" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Sox">Black Sox</a>,</b> after 8 players were banned from baseball for fixing the <a href="/wiki/1919_World_Series" title="1919 World Series">1919 World Series</a>.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/1959_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1959 Chicago White Sox season">1959 White Sox</a> were referred to as the <b>Go-Go White Sox</b> due to their speed-based offense. The period from 1950 to 1964, in which the White Sox had 15 consecutive winning seasons, is sometimes referred to as the Go-Go Era.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/1977_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1977 Chicago White Sox season">1977 team</a> were known as the <b>South Side Hitmen</b> as they contended for the division title after finishing last <a href="/wiki/1976_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1976 Chicago White Sox season">the year before</a>.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/1983_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1983 Chicago White Sox season">1983 White Sox</a> became known as the <b>Winning Ugly White Sox</b> in response to <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a> manager <a href="/wiki/Doug_Rader" title="Doug Rader">Doug Rader</a>\'s derisive comments that the White Sox "...weren\'t playing well. They\'re winning ugly."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The Sox went on to win the 1983 American League West division on September 17.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mascots">Mascots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chicago_White_Sox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mascots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Southpaw.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Southpaw.jpg/220px-Southpaw.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Southpaw.jpg/330px-Southpaw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Southpaw.jpg/440px-Southpaw.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Southpaw.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Southpaw</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_mascots" title="List of Major League Baseball mascots">List of Major League Baseball mascots</a></div>\n<p>From 1961 until 1991, lifelong Chicago resident Andrew Rozdilsky performed as the unofficial yet popular <a href="/wiki/Mascot" title="Mascot">mascot</a> "<a href="/wiki/Andy_the_Clown" title="Andy the Clown">Andy the Clown</a>" for the White Sox at the original Comiskey Park. Known for his elongated "Come on you White Sox" battle cry, Andy got his start after a group of friends invited him to a Sox game in 1960, where he decided to wear his clown costume and entertain fans in his section. That response was so positive that when he won free <a href="/wiki/1961_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1961 Chicago White Sox season">1961</a> season tickets, he decided to wear his costume to all games.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Comiskey Park ushers eventually offered free admission to Rozdilsky.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Starting in <a href="/wiki/1981_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1981 Chicago White Sox season">1981</a>, the new ownership group led by Jerry Reinsdorf introduced a twosome, called <a href="/wiki/Ribbie_and_Roobarb" class="mw-redirect" title="Ribbie and Roobarb">Ribbie and Roobarb</a>, as the official team mascots, and banned Rozdilsky from performing in the lower seating level. Ribbie and Roobarb were very unpopular, as they were seen as an attempt to get rid of the beloved Andy the Clown.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1988, the Sox got rid of Ribbie and Roobarb, and Andy The Clown was not permitted to perform in new Comiskey Park when it opened in <a href="/wiki/1991_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1991 Chicago White Sox season">1991</a>. In the early 1990s the White Sox had a cartoon mascot named, \'Waldo The White Sox Wolf\' that advertised the \'Silver and Black Pack\', the team kid\'s club at the time. The team\'s current mascot, <a href="/wiki/Southpaw_(mascot)" class="mw-redirect" title="Southpaw (mascot)">SouthPaw</a>, was introduced in 2004 to attract young fans.<sup id="cite_ref-AboutSouthpaw_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AboutSouthpaw-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fight_and_theme_songs">Fight and theme songs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chicago_White_Sox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fight and theme songs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Nancy_Faust" title="Nancy Faust">Nancy Faust</a> became the White Sox organist in 1970, a position she would hold for 40 years.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> She was one of the first ballpark organists to play pop music, and became known for her songs playing on the names of opposing players (such as <a href="/wiki/Iron_Butterfly" title="Iron Butterfly">Iron Butterfly</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/In-A-Gadda-Da-Vida" title="In-A-Gadda-Da-Vida">In-A-Gadda-Da-Vida</a>" for <a href="/wiki/Pete_Incaviglia" title="Pete Incaviglia">Pete Incaviglia</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Vickery_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vickery-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Her many years with the White Sox established her as one of the last great <a href="/wiki/Organist" title="Organist">stadium organists</a>. Since 2011, Lori Moreland has served as the White Sox organist.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Similar to the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a> with "<a href="/wiki/Sweet_Caroline" title="Sweet Caroline">Sweet Caroline</a>" (and two songs named "<a href="/wiki/Tessie" title="Tessie">Tessie</a>"), and the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a> with "<a href="/wiki/Theme_from_New_York,_New_York" title="Theme from New York, New York">Theme from New York, New York</a>", several songs have become associated with the White Sox over the years. They include:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Go,_Go-Go_White_Sox" title="Let&#39;s Go, Go-Go White Sox">"Let\'s Go Go Go White Sox"</a> by <a href="/wiki/Captain_Stubby_and_the_Buccaneers" title="Captain Stubby and the Buccaneers">Captain Stubby and the Buccaneers</a> \xe2\x80\x93 A tribute to the "Go-Go White Sox" of the late 1950s, this song serves as the unofficial fight song of the White Sox. In 2005, scoreboard operator Jeff Szynal found a record of the song and played it for a "Turn Back the Clock" game against the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a>, whom the Sox played in the <a href="/wiki/1959_World_Series" title="1959 World Series">1959 World Series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> After catcher <a href="/wiki/A._J._Pierzynski" title="A. J. Pierzynski">A. J. Pierzynski</a> hit a walk-off home run, they kept the song around as the White Sox went on to win the <a href="/wiki/2005_World_Series" title="2005 World Series">2005 World Series</a>.</li>\n<li>"<a href="/wiki/Na_Na_Hey_Hey_Kiss_Him_Goodbye" title="Na Na Hey Hey Kiss Him Goodbye">Na Na Hey Hey Kiss Him Goodbye</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Steam_(band)" title="Steam (band)">Steam</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Organist Nancy Faust played this song during the 1977 pennant race when a <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Royals" title="Kansas City Royals">Kansas City Royals</a> pitcher was pulled, and it became an immediate hit with White Sox fans.<sup id="cite_ref-Vickery_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vickery-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Faust is credited with making the song a stadium anthem and saving it from obscurity. To this day the song remains closely associated with the White Sox, who play it when the team forces a pitching change, and occasionally on Sox home runs and victories.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>"<a href="/wiki/Sweet_Home_Chicago" title="Sweet Home Chicago">Sweet Home Chicago</a>" \xe2\x80\x93 The <a href="/wiki/Blues_Brothers" class="mw-redirect" title="Blues Brothers">Blues Brothers</a> version of this <a href="/wiki/Robert_Johnson" title="Robert Johnson">Robert Johnson</a> blues standard is played after White Sox victories.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivalries">Rivalries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chicago_White_Sox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Rivalries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks
